      <p>
       <b>
        Costa Rica
       </b>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">
            ˌ
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">
            ɒ
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
          </span>
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'r' in 'rye'">
            r
           </span>
           <span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">
            iː
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
        <small class="nowrap">
         (
         <span class="unicode haudio">
          <span class="fn">
           <span style="white-space:nowrap">
            <a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Costa_Rica.ogg" title="About this sound">
             <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
            </a>
           </span>
           <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/En-us-Costa_Rica.ogg" title="En-us-Costa Rica.ogg">
            listen
           </a>
          </span>
         </span>
         )
        </small>
       </span>
       ;
       <small>
        Spanish:
       </small>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Spanish" title="Help:IPA/Spanish">
         [ˈkosta ˈrika]
        </a>
       </span>
       ; "Rich Coast"), officially the
       <b>
        Republic of Costa Rica
       </b>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="es">
         República de Costa Rica
        </span>
       </i>
       ), is a country in
       <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">
        Central America
       </a>
       , bordered by
       <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">
        Nicaragua
       </a>
       to the north,
       <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">
        Panama
       </a>
       to the southeast, the Pacific Ocean to the west, the
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">
        Caribbean Sea
       </a>
       to the east, and
       <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">
        Ecuador
       </a>
       to the south of
       <a href="/wiki/Cocos_Island" title="Cocos Island">
        Cocos Island
       </a>
       . It has a population of around 4.9 million,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in a land area of 51,060 square kilometers (19,714 square miles); over 300,000 live in the capital and largest city,
       <a href="/wiki/San_Jos%C3%A9,_Costa_Rica" title="San José, Costa Rica">
        San José
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldscapitalcities.com_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-worldscapitalcities.com-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which had a population of an estimated 333,980 in 2015.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldscapitalcities.com_7-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-worldscapitalcities.com-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Costa Rica has been known for its stable democracy in a region that has had some instability and for its highly educated workforce, most of whom speak English.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seattletimes.com_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-seattletimes.com-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The country spends roughly 6.9% of its budget (2016) on education, compared to a global average of 4.4%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seattletimes.com_8-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-seattletimes.com-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its economy, once heavily dependent on agriculture, has diversified to include sectors such as finance, corporate services for foreign companies, pharmaceuticals, and
       <a href="/wiki/Ecotourism" title="Ecotourism">
        ecotourism
       </a>
       . Many foreign companies (manufacturing and services) operate in Costa Rica's free trade zones (FTZ) where they benefit from investment and tax incentives.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cinde.org_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cinde.org-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In spite of impressive growth in the
       <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">
        Gross domestic product
       </a>
       (GDP), low
       <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">
        inflation
       </a>
       , moderate interest rates and an acceptable unemployment level, Costa Rica in 2017 was facing a
       <a href="/wiki/Market_liquidity" title="Market liquidity">
        liquidity crisis
       </a>
       due to a growing debt and budget deficit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By August 2017, the Treasury was having difficulty paying its obligations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other challenges facing the country in its attempts to improve the economy by increasing foreign investment include a poor
       <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">
        infrastructure
       </a>
       and a need to improve public sector efficiency.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.co.cr_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-news.co.cr-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Costa Rica was sparsely inhabited by indigenous people before coming under
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">
        Spanish rule
       </a>
       in the 16th century. It remained a peripheral colony of the empire until independence as part of the short-lived
       <a href="/wiki/First_Mexican_Empire" title="First Mexican Empire">
        First Mexican Empire
       </a>
       , followed by membership in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Provinces_of_Central_America" title="United Provinces of Central America">
        United Provinces of Central America
       </a>
       , from which it formally declared independence in 1847. Since then, Costa Rica has remained among the most stable, prosperous, and progressive nations in Latin America. Following the brief
       <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rican_Civil_War" title="Costa Rican Civil War">
        Costa Rican Civil War
       </a>
       , it permanently
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_of_Costa_Rica" title="Military of Costa Rica">
        abolished its army
       </a>
       in 1949, becoming one of only a few sovereign
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_without_armed_forces" title="List of countries without armed forces">
        nations without a standing army
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The country has consistently performed favourably in the
       <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">
        Human Development Index
       </a>
       (HDI), placing 69th in the world as of 2015
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Costa_Rica&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , among the highest of any Latin American nation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HDI12_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-HDI12-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It has also been cited by the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Programme" title="United Nations Development Programme">
        United Nations Development Programme
       </a>
       (UNDP) as having attained much higher
       <a href="/wiki/Human_development_(humanity)" title="Human development (humanity)">
        human development
       </a>
       than other countries at the same income levels, with a better record on human development and inequality than the median of the region.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HDI10_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-HDI10-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Costa Rica also has progressive environmental policies. It is the only country to meet all five UNDP criteria established to measure environmental sustainability.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HDI11_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-HDI11-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was ranked 42nd in the world, and third in the Americas, in the 2016
       <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Performance_Index" title="Environmental Performance Index">
        Environmental Performance Index
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPI2012_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-EPI2012-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and was twice ranked the best performing country in the
       <a href="/wiki/New_Economics_Foundation" title="New Economics Foundation">
        New Economics Foundation
       </a>
       's (NEF)
       <a href="/wiki/Happy_Planet_Index" title="Happy Planet Index">
        Happy Planet Index
       </a>
       , which measures environmental sustainability,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HPI01_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-HPI01-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HPI02_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-HPI02-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and was identified by the NEF as the
       <a href="/wiki/Environmentally_friendly" title="Environmentally friendly">
        greenest
       </a>
       country in the world in 2009.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Costa Rica plans to become a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_neutral" title="Carbon neutral">
        carbon-neutral
       </a>
       country by 2021.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By 2016, 98.1% of its electricity was generated from green sources
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       particularly
       <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">
        hydro
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">
        solar
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">
        geothermal
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">
        biomass
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Costa_Rica" title="History of Costa Rica">
        History of Costa Rica
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stone_sphere.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1533" data-file-width="1417" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Stone_sphere.jpg/220px-Stone_sphere.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Stone_sphere.jpg/330px-Stone_sphere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Stone_sphere.jpg/440px-Stone_sphere.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stone_sphere.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A stone sphere created by the
         <a href="/wiki/Diquis" title="Diquis">
          Diquis
         </a>
         culture at the
         <a href="/wiki/Museo_Nacional_de_Costa_Rica" title="Museo Nacional de Costa Rica">
          National Museum of Costa Rica
         </a>
         .
         <a href="/wiki/Stone_spheres_of_Costa_Rica" title="Stone spheres of Costa Rica">
          The sphere
         </a>
         is the icon of the country's cultural identity.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Columbian_period">
        Pre-Columbian period
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Historians have classified the
       <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">
        indigenous people
       </a>
       of Costa Rica as belonging to the
       <a href="/wiki/Intermediate_Area" title="Intermediate Area">
        Intermediate Area
       </a>
       , where the peripheries of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesoamerican" title="Mesoamerican">
        Mesoamerican
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andean" title="Andean">
        Andean
       </a>
       native cultures overlapped. More recently,
       <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">
        pre-Columbian
       </a>
       Costa Rica has also been described as part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Isthmo-Colombian_Area" title="Isthmo-Colombian Area">
        Isthmo-Colombian Area
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Stone tools, the oldest evidence of human occupation in Costa Rica, are associated with the arrival of various groups of
       <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">
        hunter-gatherers
       </a>
       about 10,000 to 7,000 years
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Before_Common_Era" title="Before Common Era">
        BCE
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Turrialba" title="Turrialba">
        Turrialba Valley
       </a>
       . The presence of
       <a href="/wiki/Clovis_culture" title="Clovis culture">
        Clovis culture
       </a>
       type spearheads and arrows from
       <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">
        South America
       </a>
       opens the possibility that, in this area, two different cultures coexisted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-precolumbian1_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-precolumbian1-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Agriculture became evident in the populations that lived in Costa Rica about 5,000 years ago. They mainly grew
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tubers" title="Tubers">
        tubers
       </a>
       and roots. For the first and second millennia BCE there were already settled farming communities. These were small and scattered, although the timing of the transition from
       <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">
        hunting
       </a>
       and gathering to agriculture as the main livelihood in the territory is still unknown.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-precolumbian2_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-precolumbian2-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The earliest use of
       <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">
        pottery
       </a>
       appears around 2,000 to 3,000 BCE. Shards of pots, cylindrical vases, platters, gourds and other forms of vases decorated with grooves, prints, and some modelled after animals have been found.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-precolumban3_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-precolumban3-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The impact of indigenous peoples on modern Costa Rican culture has been relatively small compared to other nations, since the country lacked a strong native civilization to begin with. Most of the native population was absorbed into the Spanish-speaking
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">
        colonial society
       </a>
       through inter-marriage, except for some small remnants, the most significant of which are the
       <a href="/wiki/Bribri_people" title="Bribri people">
        Bribri
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boruca_people" title="Boruca people">
        Boruca
       </a>
       tribes who still inhabit the mountains of the
       <a href="/wiki/Cordillera_de_Talamanca" title="Cordillera de Talamanca">
        Cordillera de Talamanca
       </a>
       , in the southeastern part of Costa Rica, near the frontier with
       <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">
        Panama
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_colonization">
        Spanish colonization
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The name
       <i>
        la costa rica
       </i>
       , meaning "rich coast" in the Spanish language, was in some accounts first applied by
       <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">
        Christopher Columbus
       </a>
       , who sailed to the eastern shores of Costa Rica during his final voyage in 1502,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-embassy_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-embassy-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and reported vast quantities of gold jewelry worn by natives.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lp_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-lp-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The name may also have come from conquistador
       <a href="/wiki/Gil_Gonz%C3%A1lez_D%C3%A1vila" title="Gil González Dávila">
        Gil González Dávila
       </a>
       , who landed on the west coast in 1522, encountered natives, and appropriated some of their gold.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dk_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-dk-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3168" data-file-width="4752" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg/220px-Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg/330px-Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg/440px-Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Ujarr%C3%A1s" title="Ujarrás">
          Ujarrás
         </a>
         historical site in the Orosí Valley,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartago_province" title="Cartago province">
          Cartago province
         </a>
         . The church was built between 1686 and 1693.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       During most of the colonial period, Costa Rica was the southernmost province of the
       <a href="/wiki/Captaincy_General_of_Guatemala" title="Captaincy General of Guatemala">
        Captaincy General of Guatemala
       </a>
       , nominally part of the
       <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">
        Viceroyalty of New Spain
       </a>
       . In practice, the captaincy general was a largely autonomous entity within the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">
        Spanish Empire
       </a>
       . Costa Rica's distance from the capital of the captaincy in
       <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">
        Guatemala
       </a>
       , its legal prohibition under Spanish law from trade with its southern neighbor Panama, then part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Granada" title="Viceroyalty of New Granada">
        Viceroyalty of New Granada
       </a>
       (i.e.
       <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">
        Colombia
       </a>
       ), and lack of resources such as gold and silver, made Costa Rica into a poor, isolated, and sparsely-inhabited region within the Spanish Empire.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Costa Rica was described as "the poorest and most miserable Spanish colony in all America" by a Spanish governor in 1719.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Another important factor behind Costa Rica's poverty was the lack of a significant indigenous population available for
       <a href="/wiki/Encomienda" title="Encomienda">
        encomienda
       </a>
       (forced labor), which meant most of the Costa Rican settlers had to work on their own land, preventing the establishment of large
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Hacienda" title="Hacienda">
         haciendas
        </a>
       </i>
       (plantations). For all these reasons, Costa Rica was, by and large, unappreciated and overlooked by the
       <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Spain" title="Habsburg Spain">
        Spanish Crown
       </a>
       and left to develop on its own. The circumstances during this period are believed to have led to many of the idiosyncrasies for which Costa Rica has become known, while concomitantly setting the stage for Costa Rica's development as a more egalitarian society than the rest of its neighbors. Costa Rica became a "rural democracy" with no oppressed
       <a href="/wiki/Mestizo" title="Mestizo">
        mestizo
       </a>
       or indigenous class. It was not long before Spanish settlers turned to the hills, where they found rich volcanic soil and a milder climate than that of the lowlands.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Independence">
        Independence
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Like the rest of Central America, Costa Rica never fought for independence from Spain. On September 15, 1821, after the final Spanish defeat in the
       <a href="/wiki/Mexican_War_of_Independence" title="Mexican War of Independence">
        Mexican War of Independence
       </a>
       (1810–21), the authorities in Guatemala declared the independence of all of Central America. That date is still celebrated as Independence Day in Costa Rica even though, technically, under the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Constitution_of_1812" title="Spanish Constitution of 1812">
        Spanish Constitution of 1812
       </a>
       that had been readopted in 1820,
       <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">
        Nicaragua
       </a>
       and Costa Rica had become an autonomous province with its capital in
       <a href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3n,_Nicaragua" title="León, Nicaragua">
        León
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Upon independence, Costa Rican authorities faced the issue of officially deciding the future of the country. Two bands formed, the Imperialists, defended by
       <a href="/wiki/Cartago,_Costa_Rica" title="Cartago, Costa Rica">
        Cartago
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Heredia_Province" title="Heredia Province">
        Heredia cities
       </a>
       which were in favor of joining the
       <a href="/wiki/First_Mexican_Empire" title="First Mexican Empire">
        Mexican Empire
       </a>
       , and the Republicans, represented by the cities of
       <a href="/wiki/San_Jos%C3%A9,_Costa_Rica" title="San José, Costa Rica">
        San José
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Alajuela" title="Alajuela">
        Alajuela
       </a>
       who defended full independence. Because of the lack of agreement on these two possible outcomes, the first civil war of Costa Rica occurred. The
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Ochomogo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Battle of Ochomogo (page does not exist)">
        battle of Ochomogo
       </a>
       <span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">
        (
        <a class="extiw" href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Batalla_de_Ochomogo" title="es:Batalla de Ochomogo">
         es
        </a>
        )
       </span>
       took place on the Hill of Ochomogo, located in the
       <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rican_Central_Valley" title="Costa Rican Central Valley">
        Central Valley
       </a>
       in 1823. The conflict was won by the Republicans and, as a consequence, the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Cartago,_Costa_Rica" title="Cartago, Costa Rica">
        Cartago
       </a>
       lost its status as the capital, which moved to
       <a href="/wiki/San_Jos%C3%A9,_Costa_Rica" title="San José, Costa Rica">
        San José
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="949" data-file-width="800" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg/220px-First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg/330px-First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg/440px-First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The 1849
         <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Costa_Rica" title="Coat of arms of Costa Rica">
          national coat of arms
         </a>
         was featured in the first postal stamp issued in 1862
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 1838, long after the
       <a href="/wiki/Federal_Republic_of_Central_America" title="Federal Republic of Central America">
        Federal Republic of Central America
       </a>
       ceased to function in practice, Costa Rica formally withdrew and proclaimed itself sovereign. The considerable distance and poor communication routes between
       <a href="/wiki/Guatemala_City" title="Guatemala City">
        Guatemala City
       </a>
       and the Central Plateau, where most of the Costa Rican population lived then and still lives now, meant the local population had little allegiance to the federal government in Guatemala. From colonial times to now, Costa Rica's reluctance to become economically tied with the rest of Central America has been a major obstacle to efforts for greater regional integration.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_growth">
        Economic growth
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">
        Coffee
       </a>
       was first planted in Costa Rica in 1808,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and by the 1820s, it surpassed
       <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">
        tobacco
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">
        sugar
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cacao_bean" title="Cacao bean">
        cacao
       </a>
       as a primary
       <a href="/wiki/Export" title="Export">
        export
       </a>
       . In the mid-1850s, Costa Rican coffee started to become very popular in England. Coffee production remained Costa Rica's principal source of wealth well into the 20th century, creating a wealthy class of growers, the so-called Coffee Barons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The revenue helped to modernize the country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cafeimports.com_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cafeimports.com-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Most of the coffee exported was grown around the main centers of population in the Central Plateau and then transported by
       <a href="/wiki/Bullock_cart" title="Bullock cart">
        oxcart
       </a>
       to the
       <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">
        Pacific
       </a>
       port of
       <a href="/wiki/Puntarenas" title="Puntarenas">
        Puntarenas
       </a>
       after the main road was built in 1846.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cafeimports.com_46-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-cafeimports.com-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By the mid-1850s the main market for coffee was Britain.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It soon became a high priority to develop an effective transportation route from the Central Plateau to the Atlantic Ocean. For this purpose, in the 1870s, the Costa Rican government contracted with U.S. businessman
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor_C._Keith" title="Minor C. Keith">
        Minor C. Keith
       </a>
       to build a railroad to the
       <a href="/wiki/Western_Caribbean_Zone" title="Western Caribbean Zone">
        Caribbean
       </a>
       port of
       <a href="/wiki/Lim%C3%B3n" title="Limón">
        Limón
       </a>
       ; it also connected San José to Limón. Despite enormous difficulties with construction, disease, and financing, the railroad was completed in 1890.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Some 3% of the population are Afro-Costa Ricans; most descend from
       <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">
        Jamaican
       </a>
       immigrants who worked in the construction of that railway.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       U.S. convicts, Italians and Chinese immigrants also participated in the construction project. In exchange for completing the railroad, the Costa Rican government granted Minor C. Keith large tracts of land and a lease on the train route, which he used to produce bananas and export them to the United States. As a result, bananas came to rival coffee as the principal Costa Rican export, while foreign-owned corporations (including the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Fruit_Company" title="United Fruit Company">
        United Fruit Company
       </a>
       later) began to hold a major role in the national economy and eventually became a symbol of the exploitative export economy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The major labor dispute between the peasants and the United Fruit Company (The Great Banana Strike) was a major event in the country's history and was an important step that would eventually lead to the formation of effective
       <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_Costa_Rica" title="Trade unions in Costa Rica">
        trade unions in Costa Rica
       </a>
       , as the company was required to sign a collective agreement with its workers in 1938.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">
        20th century
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Historically, Costa Rica has generally enjoyed greater peace and more consistent political stability than many of its fellow Latin American nations. Since the late 19th century, however, Costa Rica has experienced two significant periods of violence. In 1917–19, General
       <a href="/wiki/Federico_Tinoco_Granados" title="Federico Tinoco Granados">
        Federico Tinoco Granados
       </a>
       ruled as a military dictator until he was overthrown and forced into exile. The unpopularity of Tinoco's regime led, after he was overthrown, to a considerable decline in the size, wealth, and political influence of the Costa Rican military. In 1948,
       <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Figueres_Ferrer" title="José Figueres Ferrer">
        José Figueres Ferrer
       </a>
       led an
       <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rican_Civil_War" title="Costa Rican Civil War">
        armed uprising
       </a>
       in the wake of a disputed presidential election between
       <a href="/wiki/Rafael_%C3%81ngel_Calder%C3%B3n_Guardia" title="Rafael Ángel Calderón Guardia">
        Rafael Ángel Calderón Guardia
       </a>
       (who had been president between 1940 and 1944) and
       <a href="/wiki/Otilio_Ulate_Blanco" title="Otilio Ulate Blanco">
        Otilio Ulate Blanco
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       With more than 2,000 dead, the resulting 44-day
       <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rican_Civil_War" title="Costa Rican Civil War">
        Costa Rican Civil War
       </a>
       was the bloodiest event in Costa Rica during the 20th century.
      </p>
      <p>
       The victorious rebels formed a government junta that
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_of_Costa_Rica" title="Military of Costa Rica">
        abolished the military
       </a>
       altogether, and oversaw the drafting of a new constitution by a democratically elected assembly.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Having enacted these reforms, the junta transferred power to Ulate on November 8, 1949. After the
       <i>
        coup d'état
       </i>
       , Figueres became a national hero, winning the country's first democratic election under the new constitution
       <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rican_general_election,_1953" title="Costa Rican general election, 1953">
        in 1953
       </a>
       . Since then, Costa Rica has held 14 presidential elections, the latest
       <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rican_general_election,_2014" title="Costa Rican general election, 2014">
        in 2014
       </a>
       . With uninterrupted democracy dating back to at least 1948, the country is the region's most stable.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">
        Geography
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Costa_Rica_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1900" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Costa_Rica_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/220px-Costa_Rica_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Costa_Rica_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/330px-Costa_Rica_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Costa_Rica_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/440px-Costa_Rica_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Costa_Rica_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Costa Rica map of Köppen climate classification.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Costa_Rica" title="Geography of Costa Rica">
        Geography of Costa Rica
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_earthquakes_in_Costa_Rica" title="List of earthquakes in Costa Rica">
        List of earthquakes in Costa Rica
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arenal_Volcano_as_seen_from_Monteverde.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1643" data-file-width="2457" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Arenal_Volcano_as_seen_from_Monteverde.jpg/220px-Arenal_Volcano_as_seen_from_Monteverde.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Arenal_Volcano_as_seen_from_Monteverde.jpg/330px-Arenal_Volcano_as_seen_from_Monteverde.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Arenal_Volcano_as_seen_from_Monteverde.jpg/440px-Arenal_Volcano_as_seen_from_Monteverde.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arenal_Volcano_as_seen_from_Monteverde.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Arenal_Volcano" title="Arenal Volcano">
          Arenal Volcano
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Costa Rica is located on the Central American
       <a href="/wiki/Isthmus" title="Isthmus">
        isthmus
       </a>
       , lying between latitudes
       <a href="/wiki/8th_parallel_north" title="8th parallel north">
        8°
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/12th_parallel_north" title="12th parallel north">
        12°N
       </a>
       , and longitudes
       <a href="/wiki/82nd_meridian_west" title="82nd meridian west">
        82°
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/86th_meridian_west" title="86th meridian west">
        86°W
       </a>
       . It borders the
       <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">
        Caribbean Sea
       </a>
       (to the east) and the Pacific Ocean (to the west), with a total of 1,290 kilometres (800 mi) of coastline, 212 km (132 mi) on the Caribbean coast and 1,016 km (631 mi) on the Pacific. Costa Rica also borders
       <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">
        Nicaragua
       </a>
       to the north (309 km or 192 mi of border) and
       <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">
        Panama
       </a>
       to the south-southeast (330 km or 210 mi of border). In total, Costa Rica comprises 51,100 square kilometres (19,700 sq mi) plus 589 square kilometres (227 sq mi) of
       <a href="/wiki/Territorial_waters" title="Territorial waters">
        territorial waters
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The highest point in the country is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cerro_Chirrip%C3%B3" title="Cerro Chirripó">
        Cerro Chirripó
       </a>
       , at 3,819 metres (12,530 ft); it is the fifth highest peak in Central America. The highest volcano in the country is the
       <a href="/wiki/Iraz%C3%BA_Volcano" title="Irazú Volcano">
        Irazú Volcano
       </a>
       (3,431 m or 11,257 ft) and the largest lake is
       <a href="/wiki/Lake_Arenal" title="Lake Arenal">
        Lake Arenal
       </a>
       . There are 14 known volcanoes in Costa Rica, and six of them have been active in the last 75 years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The country has also experienced at least ten earthquakes of magnitude 5.7 or higher (3 of magnitude 7.0 or higher) in the last century.
      </p>
      <p>
       Costa Rica also comprises several islands.
       <a href="/wiki/Cocos_Island" title="Cocos Island">
        Cocos Island
       </a>
       (24 square kilometres or 9.3 square miles) stands out because of its distance from the continental landmass, 480 kilometres (300 mi) from
       <a href="/wiki/Puntarenas" title="Puntarenas">
        Puntarenas
       </a>
       , but
       <a href="/wiki/Isla_Calero" title="Isla Calero">
        Isla Calero
       </a>
       is the largest island of the country (151.6 square kilometres or 58.5 square miles). Over 25% of Costa Rica's national territory is protected by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SINAC" title="SINAC">
        SINAC
       </a>
       (the National System of Conservation Areas), which oversees all of the country's
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Conservation_Areas_of_Costa_Rica" title="List of Conservation Areas of Costa Rica">
        protected areas
       </a>
       . Costa Rica also possesses the greatest density of species in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">
        Climate
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Because Costa Rica is located between 8 and 12 degrees north of the
       <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">
        Equator
       </a>
       , the climate is
       <a href="/wiki/Tropical_climate" title="Tropical climate">
        tropical
       </a>
       year round. However, the country has many
       <a href="/wiki/Microclimate" title="Microclimate">
        microclimates
       </a>
       depending on elevation, rainfall, topography, and by the geography of each particular region.
      </p>
      <p>
       Costa Rica's seasons are defined by how much rain falls during a particular period. The year can be split into two periods, the dry season known to the residents as summer (
       <i>
        verano
       </i>
       ), and the rainy season, known locally as winter (
       <i>
        invierno
       </i>
       ). The "summer" or dry season goes from December to April, and "winter" or rainy season goes from May to November, which almost coincides with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Atlantic_hurricane_seasons" title="List of Atlantic hurricane seasons">
        Atlantic hurricane season
       </a>
       , and during this time, it rains constantly in some regions.
      </p>
      <p>
       The location receiving the most rain is the Caribbean slopes of the
       <a href="/wiki/Cordillera_Central_(Costa_Rica)" title="Cordillera Central (Costa Rica)">
        Cordillera Central
       </a>
       mountains, with an annual rainfall of over 5,000 mm (196.9 in). Humidity is also higher on the Caribbean side than on the Pacific side. The mean annual temperature on the coastal lowlands is around 27 °C (81 °F), 20 °C (68 °F) in the main populated areas of the Cordillera Central, and below 10 °C (50 °F) on the summits of the highest mountains.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable collapsible" style="width:90%; text-align:center; font-size:90%; line-height: 1.2em; margin:auto;">

        </td>
       </tr>
